<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer15128553782063338562">The Oxford School Shakespeare has become the preferred introduction to the literary legacy of the greatest playwright in the English language. This exclusive collection of the Bard's best works has been designed specifically for readers new to Shakespeare's rich literary legacy. Each play is presented complete and unabridged, in large print. Every book is well illustrated,</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15128553782063338562">The Oxford School Shakespeare has become the preferred introduction to the literary legacy of the greatest playwright in the English language. This exclusive collection of the Bard's best works has been designed specifically for readers new to Shakespeare's rich literary legacy. Each play is presented complete and unabridged, in large print. Every book is well illustrated, and starts with a commentary and character summary. Scene synopses and character summaries clarify confusing plots, while incisive essays explore the historical context and Shakespeare's sources. Each book ends with a complete list of Shakespeare's plays and a brief chronology of the Bard's life. The detailed explanatory notes are written clearly and positioned right next to the text--no more squinting at microscopic footnotes or flipping pages back and forth in search of endnotes!<br /> The new edition of the series features new covers and new illustrations, including both new drawings and photos from recent productions of Shakespeare's plays around the globe. In addition, the notes and the introductory material have been completely revised in line with new research and in order to make them clearer and more accessible. Finally, the entire text has been redesigned and reset to enhance readability. The new edition achieves the feat of unprecedented clarity of presentation without any cuts to the original text or the detailed explanations.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15128553782063338562">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer9875195087156184997">Julius Caesar, abridged: <br /><br />BRUTUS: I love Caesar! <br /><br />CASSIUS: He's a power-hungry bastard. I think we should kill him.<br /><br />BRUTUS: Dude, we totally should.<br /><br />DECIUS: Happy Ides of March, Caesar. Ready to go to the Senate?<br /><br />CAESAR: I dunno. My wife just had a dream about you and the rest of the senators washing their hands in my blood, so I think I'm going to call in sick today. <br /><br />DECIUS: Okay, I'll just tell the guys that you're a pussy who lets his wife tell him what to do. They'll understand. <br /><br />CAESAR: I'll get</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9875195087156184997">Julius Caesar, abridged: <br /><br />BRUTUS: I love Caesar! <br /><br />CASSIUS: He's a power-hungry bastard. I think we should kill him.<br /><br />BRUTUS: Dude, we totally should.<br /><br />DECIUS: Happy Ides of March, Caesar. Ready to go to the Senate?<br /><br />CAESAR: I dunno. My wife just had a dream about you and the rest of the senators washing their hands in my blood, so I think I'm going to call in sick today. <br /><br />DECIUS: Okay, I'll just tell the guys that you're a pussy who lets his wife tell him what to do. They'll understand. <br /><br />CAESAR: I'll get my coat. <br /><br />*Caesar skips off to the Senate, confident in the knowledge that he's in a Shakespeare play, where dreams don't predict anything and main characters never get offed*<br /><br />CAESAR: Hey, why didn't anyone tell me it was Bring A Dagger To Work Day?<br /><br />THE ENTIRE FUCKING SENATE: WE KEEL YOU! <br /><br />CASSIUS: Good, he's dead. Now to hold a huge funeral and let his best friend deliver the eulogy to the large, violence-prone mob.<br /><br />BRUTUS: Cool. Take it away, Antony!<br /><br />ANTONY: So the guys who killed Caesar aren't bad guys, really...<br /><br />CROWD: WOOOO! WE LOVE BRUTUS! <br /><br />ANTONY: ...but Caesar was generous and humble and basically god on earth, and they totally killed him in cold blood.<br /><br />CROWD: RAAAAAAA! KILL THEM ALL!!! *grabs torches and pitchforks and kills fucking everyone, including a random poet who has the same name as one of the conspirators. I'm not even joking.*<br /><br />BRUTUS: Man, ruling Rome was a lot more fun when we weren't being invaded by Octavius. <br /><br />CAESAR'S GHOST: BOOGEDY BOOGEDY BOOGEDY! AVENGE...oh, wrong play. Uh...BOOGEDY!<br /><br />BRUTUS: Oh, hey Caesar. <br /><br />CAESAR'S GHOST: Uh...that's it? Not even an "eek?" Fine, whatever. I'm going to see you a second time, by the way. BOOGEDY! <br /><br />BRUTUS: Huh. That was weird. <br /><br />CASSIUS: GOD DAMMIT WE'RE LOSING THE WAR! I AM OVER THIS SHIT. Hey you, hold my sword while I impale myself. <br /><br />SERVANT: Sure thing. <br /><br />CASSIUS: *dies*<br /><br />BRUTUS: Let's see: Rome is being destroyed, all my friends have either been killed or comitted suicide, my wife just poisoned herself, and I'm about to be captured by enemy soldiers. *turns to audience* HEY, DOES ANYONE KNOW WHAT TIME IT IS?<br /><br />AUDIENCE: SUICIDE TIME! <br /><br />BRUTUS: THAT'S RIGHT! *dies*<br /><br />THE END.</span>

      <a href="/user/show/83582-bill-kerwin" class="user" title="Bill  Kerwin">Bill  Kerwin</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=16th-17th-c-brit" class="actionLinkLite">16th-17th-c-brit</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=tudor-drama" class="actionLinkLite">tudor-drama</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1182593">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1995462709004462112"><br />In the course of teaching high school sophomores for thirty years, I have read Julius Caesar more than thirty times, and I never grow tired of its richness of detail or the complexity of its characters. Almost every year, I end up asking myself the same simple question--"Whom do I like better? Cassius or Brutus?"--and almost every year my answer is different from what it was the year before. <br /><br />On one hand, we have Cassius, the selfish, manipulative conspirator who, after the assassination, shows h</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1995462709004462112"><br />In the course of teaching high school sophomores for thirty years, I have read Julius Caesar more than thirty times, and I never grow tired of its richness of detail or the complexity of its characters. Almost every year, I end up asking myself the same simple question--"Whom do I like better? Cassius or Brutus?"--and almost every year my answer is different from what it was the year before. <br /><br />On one hand, we have Cassius, the selfish, manipulative conspirator who, after the assassination, shows himself to be an impulsive, loyal friend and an able politician, and, on the other hand, Brutus, the conscientious intellectual and lover of the republic who becomes, under the weight of his guilt, an irritatingly scrupulous moralist and an inept general more concerned with reputation than success. And then of course there is Antony: brilliant, vicious, unscrupulous, and ultimately as unknowable as a tornado. <br /><br />This is a great play about politics and human character.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1995462709004462112">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1455363177">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1455363177" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten" class="left imgcol" title="Jeffrey Keeten"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1301087352p2/3427339.jpg" alt="Jeffrey Keeten" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1455363177?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 06, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten" class="user" title="Jeffrey Keeten">Jeffrey Keeten</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/5849597-julius-caesar" title="Paperback 9780174435907" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1455363177">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15911509224629403113"><blockquote> <strong>“Cowards die many times before their deaths; <br />The valiant never taste of death but once. <br />Of all the wonders that I yet have heard, <br />It seems to me most strange that men should fear; <br />Seeing that death, a necessary end, <br />Will come when it will come.” </strong> </blockquote><br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/Julius20Caesar_zpsap29yzzn.jpg.html"> <img class="escapedImg" alt=" photo Julius20Caesar_zpsap29yzzn.jpg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1449333979i/17270172.jpg" /></a><br /><br />Beware the Ides of March. Beware to those that have aspirations to rule. You may encounter many enemies. People who will thwart your plans. People quite possibly afraid of your genius. People suffering from delusions of grandeur. <br /><br />I always say keep an</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15911509224629403113"><blockquote>
  <strong>“Cowards die many times before their deaths; <br />The valiant never taste of death but once. <br />Of all the wonders that I yet have heard, <br />It seems to me most strange that men should fear; <br />Seeing that death, a necessary end, <br />Will come when it will come.” </strong>
</blockquote><br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/Julius20Caesar_zpsap29yzzn.jpg.html">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt=" photo Julius20Caesar_zpsap29yzzn.jpg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1449333979i/17270172.jpg" /></a><br /><br />Beware the Ides of March. Beware to those that have aspirations to rule. You may encounter many enemies. People who will thwart your plans. People quite possibly afraid of your genius. People suffering from delusions of grandeur. <br /><br />I always say keep an eye on the son of your favorite squeeze. <br /><br />Marcus Junius Brutus, what a fickle man, you are running around like a plucked chicken looking for your missing head. <em>”He seems completely blind to reality, an ineffectual idealist whose idealism cannot prevent him from committing a senseless and terrible crime.”</em> You let the insidious Cassius fill your ear with dilettante, conspiratorial nonsense. <em>”Cadaverous and hungry-looking, much given to brooding, and a great reader; a scorner of sports and light diversions, a very shrewd judge of human nature, and deeply envious of those who are greater than himself.”</em> So the question remains, is Cassius the shrewd judge of character, capable of seeing the future, or is he the man consumed by jealousy who wants to see the mighty Julius Caesar fall? <br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/Brutus_zpsra4tv42g.jpg.html">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt=" photo Brutus_zpsra4tv42g.jpg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1449333979i/17270173._SY540_.jpg" /></a><br /><br />You fell for that first man of Rome, the republic is your responsibility, and all that. As it turns out, you aren’t the only dagger maestro in your family. Gaius Servilius Structus Ahala, a distant relative of yours, saved Rome from another tyrant named Spurius Maelius. Of course, that is all in the far distant past and might even be a myth, but Cassius knows the right buttons to push.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <strong>”And therefore think him as a serpent’s egg,<br />Which, hatch’d, would, as his kind, grow mischievous,<br />And kill him in the shell.”</strong>
</blockquote><br /><br />You might have said the line Brutus, but the stench of it, the green gray smoke of it, smacks of Cassius. Wouldn’t it have been more prudent to see what Caesar intended to do with his power before you stab, stab, <strong>STABBED</strong> him to death?<br /><br /><strong>“Et tu, Brute?”</strong><br /><br />That must have felt like a punch in the gut given that you had his blood all over your sword and hands at the time. Caesar’s parting guilt laden gift to you. I’m just putting a few thoughts out there in the wind. How’d you feel about Caesar putting the sausage to your mother? Did the bedposts banging against the wall feel like a drummer hammering your skull? Maybe Cassius doesn’t have to be that convincing. <br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/Gaius_Cassius_Longinus_zpsih0pwx23.jpg.html">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt=" photo Gaius_Cassius_Longinus_zpsih0pwx23.jpg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1449333979i/17270174._SY540_.jpg" /></a><br /><strong>Cassius</strong><br /><br />It must have been a real kick in the subligaculum when that hack William Shakespeare named the play after Julius Caesar. My god, man, you have four times the lines, and for most of the play Caesar is nothing more than an apparition. An annoying apparition, by the way, who keeps showing up at the most inconvenient times and saying things like, <strong>”Let loose the dogs of war.”</strong><br /><br />Letting Marc Antony live was probably a mistake. He isn’t the brightest star in the firmament, but he is a brave soldier. A good leader, but better as number two than number one. You aren’t really a mad dog killer after all, so the thought of killing Antony is like crunching on the bones of a stale dormouse. <br /><br /><blockquote>
  <em>”Of course will seem too bloody, Caius Cassius,<br />To cut the head off and then hack the limbs,<br />Like wrath in death and envy afterwards;<br />For Antony is but a limb of Caesar.<br />Let’s be sacrificers, but not butchers, Caius.<br />We all stand up against the spirit of Caesar.”</em>
</blockquote><br /><br />Magnanimous of you, Brutus. Well said, but did you think <strong>ZOINKS</strong> after Antony dropped that rap battle speech at Caesar’s funeral. <br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/Marc20Antony_zpsa1mye21o.jpg.html">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt=" photo Marc20Antony_zpsa1mye21o.jpg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1449333980i/17270175.jpg" /></a><br /><strong>Marc Antony</strong><br /><br /><blockquote>
  <strong>”Friends, Romans, countrymen, lend me your ears;<br />I come to bury Caesar, not to praise him.<br />The evil that men do lives after them;<br />The good is oft interred with their bones;<br />So let it be with Caesar. The noble Brutus<br />Hath told you Caesar was ambitious:<br />If it were so, it was a grievous fault,<br />And grievously hath Caesar answer’d it.<br />Here, under leave of Brutus and the rest–<br />For Brutus is an honourable man;<br />So are they all, all honourable men–</strong>
</blockquote><br /><br />You remember the one, right? The speech where he basically calls you a douche bag under the guise of singing your praises. <br /><br />I’m not going to talk about the disaster at the battle of Philippi. I think that might have been where the term Caesar salad came into common usage. Marc Antony and Octavius join forces and break the will of your men. We are all ready, way past ready, for you to fall on your own sword. In fact, I would have happily given you a firm Caligae to the arse if you needed a little extra encouragement. <br /><br />If you wish to see more of my most recent book and movie reviews, visit <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.jeffreykeeten.com" target="_blank">http://www.jeffreykeeten.com</a><br />I also have a Facebook blogger page at:<a rel="nofollow" href="https://www.facebook.com/JeffreyKeeten" target="_blank">https://www.facebook.com/JeffreyKeeten</a><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15911509224629403113">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_424767894">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/424767894" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3593962-kalliope" class="left imgcol" title="Kalliope"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1428482885p2/3593962.jpg" alt="Kalliope" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/424767894?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 30, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3593962-kalliope" class="user" title="Kalliope">Kalliope</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/13006.Julius_Caesar" title="Paperback 9780198320272" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=fiction-english" class="actionLinkLite">fiction-english</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=literary-classics" class="actionLinkLite">literary-classics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=classical" class="actionLinkLite">classical</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=britain" class="actionLinkLite">britain</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer424767894">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14761394159267586215">What is this play about? Is it about Julius Caesar, as the title says? Well, he is assassinated half way through the play and disappears (Act 3, scene 2). Granted, his ghost reappears later on, but it is not the ghost of the caliber of Mozart’s (and Lorenzo da Ponte’s) commanding Commendatore. JC’s ghost exists only in Brutus mind as his conscience. For even if Brutus thinks that it is the ghost’s revenge to “turn our swords toward our own stomachs”, the only time the ghost speaks is to say “I a</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14761394159267586215">What is this play about? Is it about Julius Caesar, as the title says? Well, he is assassinated half way through the play and disappears (Act 3, scene 2). Granted, his ghost reappears later on, but it is not the ghost of the caliber of Mozart’s (and Lorenzo da Ponte’s) commanding Commendatore. JC’s ghost exists only in Brutus mind as his conscience. For even if Brutus thinks that it is the ghost’s revenge to “turn our swords toward our own stomachs”, the only time the ghost speaks is to say “I am your evil spirit, Brutus”.<br /><br />JC does not seem to have a huge stature anyway. His triumph celebrated at the beginning is not Rome’s but his very own, since his victory consists of having defeated Pompey’s sons, i.e. his personal enemies and not the enemies of Rome. We also see that his wife Calpurnia has little trouble in convincing him not to go to the Senate, and only a moment later Decius easily changes his mind again and persuades him to go nonetheless. When he subsequently preaches his own steeliness to the senators (“I could be well moved if I were as you... “), he is not believable. He just seems conceited. <br /><br />So, no, I do not think it is about JC.<br /><br />May be the play is about Brutus, the most interesting of the characters and the one with the most lines. He is drawn into the plot by Cassius’s astuteness and tricks, and throughout the play we are reminded that he is acting with the good of Rome as his main objective. His famous soliloquy in Act II is a defense of the nobility of the act. But both his weakness in falling prey to Cassius conniving and the loss of empathy when he coldly dismisses the memories of his deceased and yet beloved wife (“No more, I pray you…”) detract from his being the prime candidate. No, in spite of Antony’s words at the end (“This was the noblest Roman of them all…”), he remains elusive. <br /><br />Cassius's role is that of Best Supporting Actor. <br /><br />The play ends leaving the future eerily open. From history we know what happened next and the cotemporary public must have also known it, but there is no hint in the play on which way Rome will go not even on what the alternatives are.<br /><br />Of course there are always the eternity themes that Shakespeare is so extraordinary at developing and with which his plays are always loaded, themes as Ambition, Loyalty, Omens and Destiny, etc… Analyses of these are well trodden. <br /><br />I will not venture in this fertile direction.<br /><br />In previous readings I was approaching the plays by William Shakespeare as Classics existing in the historical vacuum of eternity. But in my current protracted reading of these works, it is the parallels of the plots with contemporary events or circumstances that are interesting me greatly.<br /><br />In 1599 when the play was first performed (possibly the first in the new Globe Theatre) Queen Elizabeth was 66 but looked and acted a lot older. She had lost a lot of her glamour and the icon-making machinery had begun. The boost that the triumph over the Spaniards had brought was eroding, and new problems with Ireland were coming to the limelight. The bitter rivalry between the Earl of Essex and Lord Burghley, and later with the son Robert Cecil, was keeping courtiers at bay. The secret services were increasing their control and pressure which only contributed to a greater feeling of terror. And meanwhile, there was still no clear heir to the throne. People must have felt rather itchy about the political instability and the uncertainty that the immediate future held.<br /><br />Of course causality between events of the day and a play produced in any given period are hard to detect, let alone to prove. This is not a play-à-clef. But in choosing plots and devising how to develop them, Shakespeare must have known what would ring a bell in the minds of the public. If, when seeking entertainment, the Londoners were to choose a play over bear-biting, the play had to be engaging. The author's ability in verbalizing human passions by reminding everybody of their concerns is what makes these plays so very special.<br /><br />I see then Julius Caesar as a tragedy without a hero. And the open “what now?” with which it closes, can be better understood if we become aware of the insecurities with which contemporary audience were about to enter into the following century.<br /><br /><br />PS: Orson Welles put on a production in 1937 in which the setting was the contemporary Fascist and Nazi Europe (Caesar as Mussolini?). This is available as Audio. A GR friend recommended the modern film “Me and Orson Welles” in which it seems some of the OW original footage has been included. I have ordered this DVD but have not seen it yet. I can’t wait.<br /><br />PPS: The film disappointingly does not include any original footage of the 1937 play, and is somewhat silly.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14761394159267586215">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_247726396">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/247726396" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5253785-lyn" class="left imgcol" title="Lyn"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1428721461p2/5253785.jpg" alt="Lyn" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/247726396?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 03, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5253785-lyn" class="user" title="Lyn">Lyn</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/13006.Julius_Caesar" title="Paperback 9780198320272" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer247726396">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14827690503792919595">“Et tu, Brute?”<br /><br />These lines have haunted audiences and readers for centuries, since The Bard first presented the play, believed to be in 1599, when Shakespeare would have been 35. Bringing to life scenes from Roman history, this tragedy, more than presenting a biography of the leader, instead forms a study in loyalty, honor, patriotism and friendship.<br /><br />“Friends, Romans, countrymen, lend me your ears; <br />I come to bury Caesar, not to praise him; <br />The evil that men do lives after them, <br />The good is oft in</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14827690503792919595">“Et tu, Brute?”<br /><br />These lines have haunted audiences and readers for centuries, since The Bard first presented the play, believed to be in 1599, when Shakespeare would have been 35. Bringing to life scenes from Roman history, this tragedy, more than presenting a biography of the leader, instead forms a study in loyalty, honor, patriotism and friendship.<br /><br />“Friends, Romans, countrymen, lend me your ears; <br />I come to bury Caesar, not to praise him; <br />The evil that men do lives after them, <br />The good is oft interred with their bones, <br />So let it be with Caesar ...”<br /><br />Antony’s speech has been memorized and recited by hundreds of thousands over the centuries and still stands as a testament to subtle revenge and stubborn leadership.<br /><br />“Be patient till the last. Romans, countrymen, and lovers! Hear me for my cause, and be silent that you may hear. Believe me for mine honor, and have respect to mine honor that you may believe. Censure me in your wisdom, and awake your senses that you may the better judge. If there be any in this assembly, any dear friend of Caesar’s, to him I say that Brutus' love to Caesar was no less than his. If then that friend demand why Brutus rose against Caesar, this is my answer: not that I loved Caesar less, but that I loved Rome more”<br /><br />Like Milton’s Satan, and Shakespeare’s Edmund from King Lear, the most interesting character in the play is the villain Brutus. But was he truly a villain? He was certainly written as a sympathetic antagonist. His conflicting thoughts on loyalty and honor form the most memorable elements of one of Shakespeare’s finest plays.<br /><br /><img width="400" height="400" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1433329363i/15083765._SX540_.jpg" /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14827690503792919595">...more</a>

          </span>

  <a href="/user/show/54697-matt" class="left imgcol" title="matt"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1353271036p2/54697.jpg" alt="matt" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/233443097?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 16, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/54697-matt" class="user" title="matt">matt</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/577545.Julius_Caesar" title="Paperback 9780486268767" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/54697-matt?shelf=politik" class="actionLinkLite">politik</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/54697-matt?shelf=social-crit" class="actionLinkLite">social-crit</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/54697-matt?shelf=theatrepieces" class="actionLinkLite">theatrepieces</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/54697-matt?shelf=re-readers" class="actionLinkLite">re-readers</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/54697-matt?shelf=shakespeare" class="actionLinkLite">shakespeare</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer233443097">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4567575847909194249"><br />Re-reading it for a class I'm taking, I was surprised to see that it's not the hoary, near-cliched, armchair statesman-like story I'd snored through in high school.<br /><br />It's actually a taut, crackling, suspenseful political thriller which is more compelling, dire, complex, and profound than I'd originally noticed. <br /><br />It's about revolution, revolutionaries, and the price one pays for irrigating the tree of liberty with the blood of tyrants. You get the restless, brittle, inferiority complex of Cassius, h</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4567575847909194249"><br />Re-reading it for a class I'm taking, I was surprised to see that it's not the hoary, near-cliched, armchair statesman-like story I'd snored through in high school.<br /><br />It's actually a taut, crackling, suspenseful political thriller which is more compelling, dire, complex, and profound than I'd originally noticed. <br /><br />It's about revolution, revolutionaries, and the price one pays for irrigating the tree of liberty with the blood of tyrants. You get the restless, brittle, inferiority complex of Cassius, he of the "lean and hungry look"- sort of a cross between maybe Lenin and one of the lesser lights in Dostoevsky's possessed "Demons"; you get the stoic (not with a capital "S"), noble, rational, idealistic, practical, pragmatic, steady-as-she-goes Brutus, whose integrity and manly fortitude is challenged not only by his co-conspirators but his family and employees, and- ultimately- himself; you get pompous, regal Ceaser who doesn't know when to call it in on the Ides of March; brutal, charismatic, eloquent Marc Antony, who cleans house like nobody's buisness. <br /><br />It's not with a smile that I remark that Julius Ceasar seems to be one of those texts which seems to evidence that modern revolutions eat their young, but I was struck by how the drama of ideas, of breakable characters, plays out so vividly here. One of the spuerficial lessons we might learn is that if you are going to do violence in the name of liberty, patriotism or Reason itself you must make sure you have your emotional, physical, and ideological ducks in a row. Too often the grand sweep of incisive engagement means that the goal is ultiamtely won, success is at hand, but the fallout means sectarianism, opportunism, clashing egos turning to clashing swords turning to mingled pools of blood and friend's corpses littering the smoking ground.<br /><br /> I don't think J.C is ultimately a reactionary text at all- it's quite interesting and important to see that evne though Brutus' forces are thwarted on the battlefield, and he's met a sad death by his own hand, hoisted on the petard as it were, the finale is one of legitimate grievance (from Octavius and Antony, his fresh enemies, no less!) for one who (in his way) loved not wisely but too well....</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4567575847909194249">...more</a>

          </span>

        <a href="/review/show/60847173?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 23, 2009</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer60847173">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17586299823470879169">My tenth grade teacher killed this play, not Caesar style though, that would be the treatment my eleventh grade English teacher did in poor <em>Macbeth</em>, with lots and lots of daggers and bloodshed. I don't have a good literary reference to how <em>Julius Caesar</em> got killed by a teacher. <br /><br />Supposedly the teacher was fucking at least one guy on the football team, and she was showing signs of being knocked-up by the end of the year. So maybe she had other things on her mind. In later years I'd learn that she</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17586299823470879169">My tenth grade teacher killed this play, not Caesar style though, that would be the treatment my eleventh grade English teacher did in poor <em>Macbeth</em>, with lots and lots of daggers and bloodshed. I don't have a good literary reference to how <em>Julius Caesar</em> got killed by a teacher. <br /><br />Supposedly the teacher was fucking at least one guy on the football team, and she was showing signs of being knocked-up by the end of the year. So maybe she had other things on her mind. In later years I'd learn that she sucked as badly giving a tip to someone for delivering a pizza to her as she did at teaching literature. </span>

      <a href="/user/show/3335664-david" class="user" title="David">David</a>

        rated it

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer901872761">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17449413183123053495"><blockquote> <br />But Brutus says he was ambitious;<br />And Brutus is an honourable man…. <br /> </blockquote><br /><br /><br />I think that <em>reading</em> Shakespeare's plays does not do them justice - they aren't meant to be read, they are meant to be performed, and seen performed. However, you also miss a lot if you aren't already familiar with the context and the Shakespearean language, because of course ol' Will packs a lot into every single line.<br /><br />So, this is the famous play about the conspirators who assassinated Julius Caesar, fearing his ambition to bec</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17449413183123053495"><blockquote>
<br />But Brutus says he was ambitious;<br />And Brutus is an honourable man…. <br />
</blockquote><br /><br /><br />I think that <em>reading</em> Shakespeare's plays does not do them justice - they aren't meant to be read, they are meant to be performed, and seen performed. However, you also miss a lot if you aren't already familiar with the context and the Shakespearean language, because of course ol' Will packs a lot into every single line.<br /><br />So, this is the famous play about the conspirators who assassinated Julius Caesar, fearing his ambition to become king. Among the famous lines to which we owe this play: "Et tu, Brutus?" "Cry havoc, and let slip the dogs of war!" "Cowards die many times before their deaths; the valiant never taste of death but once." And "The fault, dear Brutus, is not in our stars, but in ourselves."<br /><br />Mark Antony's speech is probably the highlight of the play. Having just been informed of Caesar's death, and with the assassins having convinced the Roman public that they'd saved Rome from a tyrant, Mark Antony gives his famous speech which is a masterpiece of mob manipulation, turning them against the conspirators and in favor of the slain Caesar.<br /><br />The conflicts are patriotism versus friendship, loyalty versus ideals, and the taint of self-interest always present in one's motives. As a tragedy, this is one of those Shakespearean plays where almost everyone ends up falling on a sword one way or the other.<br /><br />Brutus is clearly the protagonist, but I think Mark Antony wins it.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17449413183123053495">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_784271104">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/784271104" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4635205-ken-moten" class="left imgcol" title="Ken Moten"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1347740735p2/4635205.jpg" alt="Ken Moten" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/784271104?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 07, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4635205-ken-moten" class="user" title="Ken Moten">Ken Moten</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/13007.Julius_Caesar" title="Paperback 9780743482745" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4635205-ken-moten?shelf=shakespeare-stuff" class="actionLinkLite">shakespeare-stuff</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4635205-ken-moten?shelf=history-stuff" class="actionLinkLite">history-stuff</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4635205-ken-moten?shelf=plays" class="actionLinkLite">plays</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4635205-ken-moten?shelf=biography" class="actionLinkLite">biography</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer784271104">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6621846666224674924">"<em>Artemidorus: Caesar, beware of Brutus; take heed of Cassius; come<br /> not near Casca; have an eye to Cinna; trust not Trebonius; mark<br /> well Metellus Cimber; Decius Brutus loves thee not; thou hast<br /> wrong'd Caius Ligarius. There is but one mind in all these men,<br /> and it is bent against Caesar. If thou be'st not immortal, look<br /> about you: security gives way to conspiracy. The mighty gods<br /> defend thee!</em>"<br /><br />[May 2014 update below original review]<br />"I came, I saw, I reviewed." <br /><br />THE play that made the already legend</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6621846666224674924">"<em>Artemidorus: Caesar, beware of Brutus; take heed of Cassius; come<br /> not near Casca; have an eye to Cinna; trust not Trebonius; mark<br /> well Metellus Cimber; Decius Brutus loves thee not; thou hast<br /> wrong'd Caius Ligarius. There is but one mind in all these men,<br /> and it is bent against Caesar. If thou be'st not immortal, look<br /> about you: security gives way to conspiracy. The mighty gods<br /> defend thee!</em>"<br /><br />[May 2014 update below original review]<br />"I came, I saw, I reviewed." <br /><br />THE play that made the already legendary Caesar more legendary. If we are to be accurate this is not an originally researched play, but an adaption from Plutarch's biography of Caesar and Brutus. Like all of Shakespeare's historical plays, it is played for dramatic depth more than actual educational value. <br /><br />"<em>Cassius: Men at some time are masters of their fates:<br />The fault, dear Brutus, is not in our stars,<br />But in ourselves, that we are underlings.</em>"<br /><br />As for Julius Caesar himself, I find myself in constant conflict over his legacy. He was a populist who really cared for the ordinary poor and working-class people of Rome and I tend to like politicians like that. On the other hand, he was an autocrat that viciously trampled over much of Roman law and helped to destroy what was left of Rome's representative government (which, to be fair, was dominated by soulless oligarchs--and Cicero), not to mention plunging Rome into civil war just to gain more dictatorial power. Now he was not the first or second "populari" to attempt this, but he had the best cash flow (somewhat) and the best military success (in spades) which insured Rome would never be a republic again and that rulers of the Empire and after it would literally bare his name (Kaiser or Czar anyone?). <br /><br />"<em>Caesar: Cowards die many times before their deaths;<br />The valiant never taste of death but once.<br />Of all the wonders that I yet have heard,<br />It seems to me most strange that men should fear;<br />Seeing that death, a necessary end,<br />Will come when it will come.</em>"<br /><br />This play has, like so many other Shakespeare plays, entered the modern lexicon which is no small feat, considering a lot of one-liners regarding Caesar were already well established by the time this play was established. Nevertheless, this is one of the Bard's great plays and one the message of power, envy, and honor does not grow old with time.<br /><br />[Update: 5/17/2014]After going through this play again I realized that I forgot to mention another vital aspect of it. This play, while called "Julius Caesar" is about two different duo's that were brought together because of Caesar. Cassius and Marcus Brutus would not have cared much about each other if it were not for Caesar's existence. As soon as Caesar is killed, their relationship looses its significance. Meanwhile, Caesar's death brings his nephew Octavius and his best friend Mark Antony together to avenge him and when that deep is over...well you will have to check out <a title="Antony and Cleopatra by William Shakespeare" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/104837.Antony_and_Cleopatra">Antony and Cleopatra</a> to find out what happens. This play is not as much about Caesar than it shows how both sides of his legacy that I spoke of earlier reflected on people.<br /><br />"<em>Cassius: How many ages hence<br />Shall this our lofty scene be acted over,<br />In states unborn and accents yet unknown!</em>"</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6621846666224674924">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_209934558">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/209934558" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1022982-brad" class="left imgcol" title="Brad"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442484575p2/1022982.jpg" alt="Brad" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/209934558?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 07, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1022982-brad" class="user" title="Brad">Brad</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12657938-julius-caesar" title="ebook 9781613060728" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=faves" class="actionLinkLite">faves</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=shakespeare" class="actionLinkLite">shakespeare</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=one-of-the-greats" class="actionLinkLite">one-of-the-greats</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer209934558">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6306426702590519058">Whenever I read Shakespeare, I always find myself longing to be back in Rome watching the assassination of Caesar. So I do just that. <br /><br />I read Hamlet for class, and I immediately pick up Caesar. I read one of the plays I've been meaning to get to, and I immediately pick up Caesar. I catch a late night TV showing of Much Ado About Nothing or Othello, and I immediately pick up Ceasar. It feels like home to me. <br /><br />It contains the elements that make Shakespeare's great plays great (at least to me). Death</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6306426702590519058">Whenever I read Shakespeare, I always find myself longing to be back in Rome watching the assassination of Caesar. So I do just that. <br /><br />I read Hamlet for class, and I immediately pick up Caesar. I read one of the plays I've been meaning to get to, and I immediately pick up Caesar. I catch a late night TV showing of Much Ado About Nothing or Othello, and I immediately pick up Ceasar. It feels like home to me. <br /><br />It contains the elements that make Shakespeare's great plays great (at least to me). Death as the great equalizer. The corruption of power. Errors in judgement. The indecision that "goodness" necessitates. The supernatural as an inescapable motivator. The manipulative power of speech.<br /><br />It's an old friend, is Julius Caesar. My son's middle name is Cesar, and at least part of the reason for that is my love of this play. It is always on my mind, even when I am not reading other Shakespeare. Yet I cannot watch the Brando/Mason film of the play (too daunting); and I've never actually seen it staged (when I wasn't involved). <br /><br />I want to play Brutus some day soon. Then maybe Cassius before it's too late. And then Caesar when my hair is all grey. Maybe by then Milos Ernest Cesar can play Antony. Now that would be cool. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6306426702590519058">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_157144748">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/157144748" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2973969-gorfo" class="left imgcol" title="Gorfo"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1370016023p2/2973969.jpg" alt="Gorfo" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/157144748?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 23, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2973969-gorfo" class="user" title="Gorfo">Gorfo</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/760566.Julius_Caesar" title="Paperback 9780451526892" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Shakespeare Lovers!</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2973969-gorfo?shelf=historical-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2973969-gorfo?shelf=classic" class="actionLinkLite">classic</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2973969-gorfo?shelf=my-favorite-character-died" class="actionLinkLite">my-favorite-character-died</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2973969-gorfo?shelf=plays" class="actionLinkLite">plays</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer157144748">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2994007907890700653">I didn't expect to like Julius Caesar. For some reason I expected it to be one of Shakespeare's histories. Nevertheless, it soon became clear to me that I had stumbled upon an utterly enthralling tragedy. After reading Julius Caesar I've come to realize that there is no way on earth that I will ever be able to pick my favorite Shakespeare play! It just isn't possible! How could one man create so much amazing work (of course there is speculation about whether he wrote it all, but I don't care muc</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2994007907890700653">I didn't expect to like Julius Caesar. For some reason I expected it to be one of Shakespeare's histories. Nevertheless, it soon became clear to me that I had stumbled upon an utterly enthralling tragedy. After reading Julius Caesar I've come to realize that there is no way on earth that I will ever be able to pick my favorite Shakespeare play! It just isn't possible! How could one man create so much amazing work (of course there is speculation about whether he wrote it all, but I don't care much for doubting Thomases). <br /><br />Julius Caesar is not so much about Caesar as it is about those "less" than Casear. It clearly displays how envy can turn to murder, how ambition can end in death, how goodwill can be taken as something else. The plot to kill Caesar is simple, swift, easy even, but you can't kill Caesar and get away with it. Brutus killed Caesar for the good of the Rome, but the others killed for themselves, for greed, for ambitions sake. Julius Caesar is one of the best Shakespearean plays I have read. More Shakespeare now! I just can't get enough!</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2994007907890700653">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_542051730">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/542051730" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4487924-ben" class="left imgcol" title="Ben"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1393033867p2/4487924.jpg" alt="Ben" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/542051730?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 21, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4487924-ben" class="user" title="Ben">Ben</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/13006.Julius_Caesar" title="Paperback 9780198320272" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4487924-ben?shelf=english-lit" class="actionLinkLite">english-lit</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4487924-ben?shelf=historical-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4487924-ben?shelf=plays" class="actionLinkLite">plays</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer542051730">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer696281654548812732"><blockquote>He said, “This is what the king who will reign over you will claim as his rights: He will take your sons and make them serve with his chariots and horses, and they will run in front of his chariots. Some he will assign to be commanders of thousands and commanders of fifties, and others to plow his ground and reap his harvest, and still others to make weapons of war and equipment for his chariots. He will take your daughters to be perfumers and cooks and bakers. He will take the best of your fiel</blockquote></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText696281654548812732"><blockquote>He said, “This is what the king who will reign over you will claim as his rights: He will take your sons and make them serve with his chariots and horses, and they will run in front of his chariots. Some he will assign to be commanders of thousands and commanders of fifties, and others to plow his ground and reap his harvest, and still others to make weapons of war and equipment for his chariots. He will take your daughters to be perfumers and cooks and bakers. He will take the best of your fields and vineyards and olive groves and give them to his attendants. He will take a tenth of your grain and of your vintage and give it to his officials and attendants. Your male and female servants and the best of your cattle[c] and donkeys he will take for his own use. He will take a tenth of your flocks, and you yourselves will become his slaves. When that day comes, you will cry out for relief from the king you have chosen, but the Lord will not answer you in that day.” - I Samuel 8:11-18</blockquote><br />The Ides of March, described as the middle day of the month in the Roman calendar, represented a day for settling debts. On this day, Marcus Brutus says that great Caesar paid "ambition's debt" in the most infamous assassination in world history. Like in so many of his other plays, Shakespeare exercises his genius in studying these characters and events from all sides and virtually eliminates our chance to concretely label anyone as wholly good or wholly bad, infallible hero or wasted villain. With passionate dialogue and lines resounding down through the ages, Shakespeare portrays the great and regrettably incurable dilemma of civilization - the corrupting faculty of power and the slippery slope of good intentions.<br /><br />Shakespeare designs Julius Caesar as an immortal among men, the colossally powerful Roman conqueror whose history has manifested into mythical proportions and credits him with immortal ancestry. His companion, Mark Antony, speaks with the tongue of a serpent and Marcus Brutus, Rome's favorite son, known for his honor and noble heart, bears responsibility for his murder despite the many hands stained with his blood. Shakespeare intends that a fourth entity not go unnoticed. Perhaps the citizens of Rome, the mob, wield the most influence to these events and conduct the course of history like a fiery muse.<br /><br />Yet as these entities intertwine with one another, Shakespeare carefully gives each one their full dimension while simultaneously developing a schism of sorts within each individual. The opposition between Brutus and Antony illustrate the dichotomy within Caesar himself - man or powerfully ambitious god? Antony seeks vengeance for a wronged man while Brutus seeks balance to Roman political power. In their speeches at Caesar's funeral, we hear Brutus condemn a position, an ambitious dictator, while declaring love for the man. And Antony glorifies a human man unjustly murdered. Also, to hear Caesar speak in the third person throughout his short role in the play, one must either think him crazy or divided, as if he stands outside himself speaking for the man who will act on his behalf. He imparts wise anecdotes and speaks often selflessly but, like the mob, falters between convictions easily at advise from different people. Like an honorable man, he declines the people's offered crown but grimaces like an ambitious godhead denying his right for the sake of his reputation with others in government. His position imprisons the human man who speaks his mind but ultimately must do what Rome expects of him.<br /><br />Antony, with his brilliantly manipulative tongue and detestable tactics, regarding Lepidus for example, admirably seeks to avenge Caesar's death. Do we respect his moral conviction or abhor his slimy character? If his words with the conspirators after Caesar's murder, pre-sanctioned in truce by his servant with his safety fully dependent on the honorable word of Brutus, embracing them as friends, and his words at the funeral, repeating Brutus' nature as an "honorable man" while slandering him for accusing Caesar for ambition, do not reveal to the audience a two-faced coward, I maintain that Shakespeare did all he could to illustrate the character. Consider Antony's absence during the murder and for most of the play before its occurrence. One might argue that Antony, though not accomplice to the act, desired its occurrence for his own benefit. He did nothing before it happened, spoke carressingly to save himself after it happened and manipulated the mob with words rather than act nobly at Caesar's funeral. But Antony then valiantly unleashes his armies in defense of the fallen Caesar and with all the pomp of political admiration.<br /><br />Cassius, though like a tempter, would not falter under an interrogation regarding his love of Rome and his belief in the Republic free from a dictator. Though he disrespected Brutus by pestering him, and bears the sin of murder, one might respect his wide breadth of love and service to his country and ideology.<br /><br />Marcus Brutus - oh, Brutus - the true victim of tragic mischief, lived as the noblest of Romans, protecting a reputation of humble civil service even unto the funeral when he implores the mob to use the murder weapon to kill him should his death serve his country. One could argue that Cassius' desperation in recruiting Brutus to the conspiracy stemmed from Brutus' notoriety in Roman society. Brutus bore the task with a sickened heart after accepting Cassius' reasons for its execution in his mind. He embodies two adverse contagions: the moral heart and the ideological mind. Alas, I sympathize with a murderer! an honorable man who, in his weakness, succumbed to the dramatized logic of ideology! Shall men condemn Brutus the assassin or honor Brutus the heart of Rome?<br /><br />While Cassius woos Brutus to the conspiracy, Brutus notes; "...for the eye sees not itself / But by reflection, by some other things" (Act I Scene II). Shakespeare planted these lines, from the mouth of nobility and integrity, to set the tone for the rest of the play. Each angle - Caesar, Brutus, Antony, and the mob - see reflections of themselves and by these reflections know themselves. During the overnight storm preceding the assassination the next morning, Calphernia, Caesar's wife, hails it as a omen of doom on Caesar's life. Yet is the storm the doom of Caesar or the people of Rome under his dictatorship? Brutus' wife attempts to console him and notes his ailing health during the storm. And to the conspirators, coming and going between homes before the senate session, the storm could signify their intent or perhaps its unforeseen consequences. In many cases, the storm, as an example, represents the reflection which the eye sees of itself - to Brutus, his conflicted conscience and declining health, to Caesar, his demise, to Cassius and the conspirators, their murderous intent. Yet the reflection shows destruction, fear, violence and calamity for each of the players. Caesar's blood stains all.<br /><br />As war ensues between the two camps of Brutus and Antony, and prototypical Shakespearean misunderstandings spark the demise of certain characters, the audience surely thinks of justice. As one cannot forgive someone on behalf of someone else, Antony cannot revenge himself on Brutus and Cassius on behalf of Caesar - nor does he care. Antony has every intention of claiming power behind Octavius, Caesar's son, and securing his own power in Roman government in the next regime. While Brutus and Cassius fall on their own swords, honorably holding to their ideals, but suffering a sort of true and justified vengeance for killing Caesar. In this end, they suffer death for committing murder but retain their honor on the battlefield, reconciling the internal struggle within their hearts.<br /><br />But the people! Oh, the fical swaying mob quick to abandon the reasoning of Brutus for the manipulative power of Antony! The audience gazes on these citizens and observes a reflection of itself. Shakespeare, you clever nuisance! One must question their stance in these events. What sort of curses or defenses would you shout to Brutus or Antony? Would you fling the crown over Caesar's brow? Would you raise your dagger to promote the republic? Would you scurry about in search of secure power in the aftermath?<br /><br />Though some urged Shakespeare to stage his histories as propaganda, specifically playing on sentiments for and against particular figures, <em>Julius Caesar</em> lasts as a work lacking such motivation and pursues an examination of the good and bad within each character while beckoning the audience to question their own ideas and politics. Shakespeare presents men struggling to perfect a civilization which many look back to as the most powerful pinnacle of society in the history of the western world. But these are but men - full of honorable intentions, susceptible to corruption and confused by lofty ideals.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="696281654548812732">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer8602136051291427818">The first thing that comes to my mind when thinking about this play is the beginning of that famous speech "Friends, Romans, Countrymen!" Yes, Mark Anthony really made quite a speech there! You have to love the fact that the play that is in many ways philosophical can also be dramatic.<br /><br />This is one of my favourite plays by Shakespeare mainly I think that it describes perfectly that burning ice within that we sometimes call ambition and sometimes lust for power. It was this examination of power, co</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8602136051291427818">The first thing that comes to my mind when thinking about this play is the beginning of that famous speech "Friends, Romans, Countrymen!" Yes, Mark Anthony really made quite a speech there! You have to love the fact that the play that is in many ways philosophical can also be dramatic.<br /><br />This is one of my favourite plays by Shakespeare mainly I think that it describes perfectly that burning ice within that we sometimes call ambition and sometimes lust for power. It was this examination of power, conspiracy, duty, patriotism, friendship and politics that captured my attention. In addition, I always liked the historical figures that happen to be the main characters in this play. That may be the reason why I enjoyed Antony and Cleopatra as well.<br /><br />Despite what its title suggests, this play doesn't focus on Julius Ceaser, but rather on the people surrounding him. However, that is not of much importance as other characters (Mark Anthony, Brutus, Cassius) are so well developed and fascinating.<br /><br />"??? ?? ??????" </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a href="/review/show/54919660?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 05, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1639098-mitchell" class="user" title="Mitchell">Mitchell</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/652272.Julius_Caesar" title="Paperback 9781903436219" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer54919660">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11007088612220959368">book club choice<br /><br />This time around I was struck by a few things:<br /><br />1- How much this play is about the power of rhetoric, especially rhetoric used to persuade. Cassius persuading Brutus, Antony persuading the crowd. Brutus' lack of rhetoric that indicates his guilelessness. Compare his flat-footed speech at the funeral to Antony's brilliant oration. The rhetoric defines the character. <br /><br />2- This is a play of what happens after giants fall. Octavius/Antony and Cassius/Brutus are sloppy seconds that cannot</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11007088612220959368">book club choice<br /><br />This time around I was struck by a few things:<br /><br />1- How much this play is about the power of rhetoric, especially rhetoric used to persuade. Cassius persuading Brutus, Antony persuading the crowd. Brutus' lack of rhetoric that indicates his guilelessness. Compare his flat-footed speech at the funeral to Antony's brilliant oration. The rhetoric defines the character. <br /><br />2- This is a play of what happens after giants fall. Octavius/Antony and Cassius/Brutus are sloppy seconds that cannot fill the vacuum left by the loss of Caesar. Shakespeare doesn't define what made Caesar great, in fact he undermines that idea by describing his epilepsy and showing him fearful of going to the Capitol, giving in to Calpurnia's urging. But that is just brilliant coloring of Shakespeare's. We know that, to quote Hamlet, 'What a falling off was there!' With Caesar gone, the world is run by very petty men who are either noble but ineffective (Brutus) or ambitious (Octavius) or just mean-spirited (Cassius)<br /><br />3- What a tight play this is. It rockets ahead from the first scene to the last!</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11007088612220959368">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer154944772020021023"><strong>Note: I dedicate this humorous review to Mr. Grooms, my 10th grade English teacher who not only taught me how to appreciate Shakespeare but introduced me to the writings of John LeCarre, John Hershey, and Shirley Jackson. But he gets minus points for making me read <em>Silas Marner.</em></strong><br /><br />I read William Shakespeare's classic play <em>Julius Caesar</em> in high school as required reading, so my review is delivered in the viewpoint of the ancient high school me...<br /><br />Our high school English teacher made us read <em>Julius Ca</em></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText154944772020021023"><strong>Note: I dedicate this humorous review to Mr. Grooms, my 10th grade English teacher who not only taught me how to appreciate Shakespeare but introduced me to the writings of John LeCarre, John Hershey, and Shirley Jackson. But he gets minus points for making me read <em>Silas Marner.</em></strong><br /><br />I read William Shakespeare's classic play <em>Julius Caesar</em> in high school as required reading, so my review is delivered in the viewpoint of the ancient high school me...<br /><br />Our high school English teacher made us read <em>Julius Caesar</em>. I thought I would hate it but it was pretty cool. Lots of fights, knives, and cool speeches. Even the dudes in the local gang thought it was pretty cool. So I decided to read more Shakespeare and chose <em>Romeo and Juliet</em>. Not so cool. Lots of cuddling and no sex. Shakespeare just copied <em>West Side Story</em> which had better fights and cooler dancing. So I told my teacher I wanted a Shakespeare play that had more sex and violence. All he said was go read King Lear.<br /><br /></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer14354632964464499795">When we study texts we learn to look deeper into a word. Of course, that depends on the teacher and personal interest. Literature studies is one of my favourite hobbies. I always like to see what is behind a word and draw it out.<br /><br />Julius Caesar tested my abilities. It also unviersally made me understand that everyone is ambiguous in nature. It changed the way I relate to people. Surprisingly, a hard hitting tragedy made me learn to forgive whole heartedly. <br /><br />This play increased my thinking depth and</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14354632964464499795">When we study texts we learn to look deeper into a word. Of course, that depends on the teacher and personal interest. Literature studies is one of my favourite hobbies. I always like to see what is behind a word and draw it out.<br /><br />Julius Caesar tested my abilities. It also unviersally made me understand that everyone is ambiguous in nature. It changed the way I relate to people. Surprisingly, a hard hitting tragedy made me learn to forgive whole heartedly. <br /><br />This play increased my thinking depth and elevated my love for literature. Since then, I've been a hardcore Shakespeare fan. Though he has this unquenching thirst to complicate words in his writing, I love him, because I have the same thirst when I write. :) Things too simple sometimes read like propaganda. <br /><br />With Shakespeare, you can work yourself to thinking you are dumb, just by getting fooled by the thin complexity. It just needs a whole-hearted reading. I always love watching Shakespearian plays. I guess that tells you enough about my view on it. <br /><br />Love,<br />Sam. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14354632964464499795">...more</a>
